$\{\alpha\}$ are production coefficients and $\{\beta\}$ are destruction coefficients. Their use in the differential equations helps in visual checking and bookkeeping of terms

The probability ratio R of path $^{230}\mathrm{Th}(\mathfrak{n},\mathfrak{Y})^{231}\mathrm{Th}$ to path $^{232}\mathrm{Th}$

$(n,2n)^{231}\mathrm{Th}$ (Fig. 3.5-5) is

$$
R = \frac {\overline {{\sigma}} _ {0 0} N _ {0 0}}{\overline {{\sigma}} _ {O 2 N} N _ {O 2}}
$$

For N/N= 10-6 (1 ppm) mentioned above, get

$$
\begin{array}{l} R = 1 0 ^ {- 6} \times \frac {7 \times 1 0 ^ {- 1 0}}{7 \times 1 0 ^ {- 1 1}} = 1 0 ^ {- 5} \text {i n c o r e} \\ = 1 0 ^ {- 6} \times \frac {6 \times 1 0 ^ {- 1 1}}{1 \times 1 0 ^ {- 1 3}} = 6 \times 1 0 ^ {- 4} \text {i n b l a n k e t} \\ \end{array}
$$

Thus the $^{230}\mathrm{Th}(n,\gamma)$ $^{231}\mathrm{Th}$ path can be ignored.

In the destruction of $\mathbf{N}_{13}$ , $\lambda_{13} = 3.0 \times 10^{-7} \mathrm{s}^{-1}$ far exceeds $\phi \sigma_{13}$ : in the core by a factor of near 30; in the blanket, by three magnitudes. In the destruction of $\mathbf{N}_{22}$ , $\delta_{22} \phi$ far exceeds $\lambda_{22}$ and thus becomes the governing time constant (of about 10 mos.) in the approach to equilibrium concentration.

Taking La Place transforms of Equations 3.5.4-4 through -6

$$
\begin{array}{l} s ^ {n} _ {2 2} - 1) _ {2 2} ^ {0} = \alpha_ {2 3} n _ {2 3} + \alpha_ {1 1} n _ {1 1} + \alpha_ {1 3} n _ {1 3} - \beta_ {2 2} n _ {2 2} \\ u _ {2 2} = \frac {1}{s + b} _ {2 2} \left\{n _ {2 2} ^ {0} + \alpha_ {2 3} n _ {2 3} + \alpha_ {1 1} n _ {1 1} + \alpha_ {1 3} u _ {1 3} \right\} \\ s \mathcal {U} _ {1 1} - \mathcal {U} _ {1 1} ^ {0} = \alpha_ {0 2 2} \mathcal {U} _ {0 2} - \beta_ {1 1} \mathcal {U} _ {1 1} \\ M _ {1 1} = \frac {1}{s + B _ {1 1}} \left[ H _ {1 1} ^ {0} + \alpha_ {0 2} H _ {0 2} \right] \\ s M _ {1 3} - M _ {1 3} ^ {0} = x _ {0 2} y _ {0 2} - y _ {1 3} M _ {1 3} \\ \eta_ {1 3} = \frac {1}{s + \beta_ {1 3}} \left\{1. 0 _ {1 3} + \alpha_ {0 2 r} \eta_ {0 2} \right\} \\ \end{array}
$$

Assuming $\mathbb{N}_i$ constant $= \mathbb{N}_i$ for 02 and 23, then $\mathcal{M}_{02}(s) = \frac{1}{s}\mathcal{M}_{02}^{c}$ .

$$
n _ {2 3} (s) = \frac {1}{3} n _ {2 3} ^ {c}, \quad \text {a n d s u b s t i t u t i n g , g e t}
$$

$$
\begin{array}{l} \eta_ {2 2} (s) = \frac {1}{s + \beta_ {2 2}} \left\{l _ {2 2} ^ {0} + \frac {\alpha_ {2 3} \eta_ {2 3} ^ {0}}{s} + \frac {\alpha_ {1 1}}{s + \beta_ {1 1}} \eta_ {1 1} ^ {0} \right. \\ + \frac {d _ {1 3}}{s + \beta_ {1 3}} \left[ n _ {1 3} ^ {0} + \frac {\alpha_ {0 2 5} \cdot n _ {c 2} ^ {c}}{s} \right] \Bigg \} \\ \end{array}
$$

Now initially one would have

$$
m _ {1 3} ^ {c} = m _ {1 1} ^ {0} = m _ {2 2} ^ {0} = c
$$

Then

$$
\begin{array}{l} n _ {2 2} = \frac {1}{s (s + \beta_ {2 2})} \left\{x _ {2 3} n _ {2 3} ^ {c} + \frac {\alpha_ {1 1}}{s - \beta_ {1 1}} x _ {C Z N} n _ {C 2} ^ {c} \right. \\ + \frac {x _ {1 3}}{s + 3 , 3} x _ {0 2 r} i _ {0 2} ^ {c} \\ \end{array}
$$

Normalizing $^{232}\mathrm{U}$ to $^{233}\mathrm{U}$ concentration one has

$$
\begin{array}{l} \frac {N _ {2 2}}{N _ {2 3}} = \frac {\alpha_ {2 3} (1 - e ^ {- \beta_ {2 2} t})}{\alpha_ {2 2}} \\ + \frac {\alpha_ {1 1} \alpha_ {2 2} \left(\frac {N _ {C 2}}{N _ {2 3}}\right)}{\beta_ {1 1} \beta_ {2 2}} \left\{\frac {\beta_ {1 1} - \beta_ {2 2} + \beta_ {2 2} e ^ {- \beta_ {1 1} t} - \beta_ {1 1} e ^ {- \beta_ {2 2} t}}{\beta_ {1 1} - \beta_ {2 2}} \right\} \\ + \frac {\alpha_ {1 3} \alpha_ {0 2 5} (\frac {N _ {2}}{N _ {2 3}})}{\beta_ {1 3} \beta_ {2 2}} \left\{\frac {\beta_ {1 3} - \beta_ {2 2} + \beta_ {2 2} e ^ {- \beta_ {1 3} t} - \beta_ {1 3} e ^ {- \beta_ {2 2} t}}{\beta_ {1 3} - \beta_ {2 2}} \right\} \\ = x _ {2 3} \left[ \frac {1 - e ^ {- \beta_ {2 2} r}}{\beta_ {2 2}} \right] \\ + \alpha_ {1 1} \alpha_ {c 2 N} \left(\frac {N _ {c 2}}{N _ {2 3}}\right) \left\{\frac {\left[ \frac {1 - e ^ {- \beta_ {2 2} r}}{\beta_ {2 L}} \right] - \left[ \frac {1 - e ^ {- \beta_ {1 1} t}}{\beta_ {1 1}} \right]}{\beta_ {1 1} - \beta_ {2 2}} \right\} \\ + x _ {1 3} \alpha_ {0 2 5} \left(\frac {N _ {0 2}}{N _ {2 3}}\right) \left\{\frac {\left[ \frac {1 - e ^ {- \beta_ {2 2} t}}{\beta_ {2 2}} \right] - \left[ \frac {1 - e ^ {- \beta_ {1 3} t}}{\beta_ {1 3}} \right]}{\beta_ {1 3} - \beta_ {2 2}} \right\} \\ \end{array}
$$

Setting $G\left( {3}_{i}\right)  = \frac{1 - {e}^{-{\beta }_{i}t}}{{3}_{i}}$

and

$\frac{{N}_{22}}{{N}_{23}} = {\alpha }_{23}{G}^{\prime }\left( {s}_{22}\right)  + {\alpha }_{11}{\alpha }_{02n}\left( \frac{{N}_{C2}}{{N}_{23}}\right)  + \left( {{\beta }_{11},{\beta }_{22}}\right)$

$$
+ \alpha_ {1 3} x _ {c 2 Y} \left(\frac {N _ {0 2}}{N _ {2 3}}\right) H \left(S _ {i 3}, S _ {i 2}\right) \tag {3.5.4-7}
$$

We can now explore $\mathbb{N}_{22} / \mathbb{N}_{23}$ buildup as a function of $\mathbb{N}_{02} / \mathbb{N}_{23}$ , i.e.

$$
[ ^ {2 3 2} U ] / [ ^ {2 3 3} U ] a s f (^ {2 3 2} \pi_ {1} / ^ {2 3 3} U)
$$

To calculate the radioactivity we construct and solve the decay equations for the $^{232}\mathrm{U}$ daughters in Figure 3.5-4. $^{224}\mathrm{Ra}, ^{220}\mathrm{Rn}, ^{216}\mathrm{Po}$ , and $^{212}\mathrm{Pb}$ all decay relatively instantaneously, so they can be skipped. For a fuel outside the reactor with a given $^{232}\mathrm{U}$ concentration one then has

$$
2 3 2 _ {U} \xrightarrow {7 2 y} 2 2 8 _ {\text {T h}} \xrightarrow {1 . 9 y} 2 1 2 _ {\text {B i}} \xrightarrow [ ]{6 0. 6 m} 2 0 8 _ {\text {T l}} \xrightarrow [ ]{3 m} 2. 6 \mathrm {K e V}
$$

For purpose of simplification, we take the $^{232}\mathrm{U}$ concentration here as constant. The differential equations are then

$$
\frac {d N _ {0 8}}{d T} = \lambda_ {2 2} N _ {2 2} - \lambda_ {0 8} N _ {0 8}
$$

$$
\frac {d \mathbb {N} _ {3 2}}{d t} = \lambda_ {0 8} \mathbb {N} _ {0 8} - \lambda_ {3 2} \mathbb {N} _ {3 2}
$$

$$
\frac {d \mathrm {N} _ {1 8}}{d \mathrm {T}} = 0. 3 6 \lambda_ {3 2} \mathrm {N} _ {3 2} - \lambda_ {1 8} \mathrm {N} _ {1 8}
$$

where

$$
N _ {2 2} = \text {c c n c e n t r a t i o n o f} \begin{array}{l} 2 3 2 \\ 9 2 \end{array}
$$

$$
N _ {O 8} = \quad \text {"} \quad \text {"} \quad \text {"} \quad \text {"} \quad \text {"} \quad \text {"} \quad \text {"} \quad \text {"} \quad \text {"} \quad \text {"} \quad \text {"} \quad \text {"} \quad \text {"} \quad \text {"} \quad \text {"} \quad \text {"} \quad \text {"} \quad \tag {228}
$$

$$
\mathrm {N} _ {3 2} = \quad \text {"} \quad \text {"} \quad \text {"} \quad \text {"} \quad \text {"} \quad \text {"} \quad \text {"} \quad \text {"} \quad \text {"} \quad \text {"} \quad \text {"} \quad \text {"} \quad \text {"} \quad \text {"} \quad \text {"} \quad \text {"} \quad \text {"} \quad \tag {83}
$$

$$
N _ {1 8} = \quad \text {"} \quad \text {"} \quad \text {"} \quad \text {"} \quad \text {"} \quad \text {"} \quad \text {"} \quad \text {"} \quad \text {"} \quad \text {"} \quad \text {"} \quad \text {"} \quad \text {"} \quad \text {"} \quad \text {"} \quad \text {"} \quad \text {"} \quad \tag {208}_{T1}
$$

$$
\lambda_ {2 2} = \ln 2 / 7 2 y = 3. 1 x 1 0 ^ {- 1 0} s ^ {- 1}
$$

$$
\lambda_ {0 8} = \ln 2 / 1. 9 y = 1. 2 ^ {\prime} x 1 0 ^ {- 8} s ^ {- 1}
$$

$$
\lambda_ {3 2} = \ln 2 / 6 0. 6 m = 1. 9 x 1 0 ^ {- 4} s ^ {- 1}
$$

$$
\lambda_ {1 8} = \ln 2 / 3 m = 3. 9 x 1 0 ^ {- 3} s ^ {- 1}
$$

Taking Laplace transforms

$$
s M _ {1 8} - M _ {1 8} ^ {\circ} = 0. 3 6 \lambda_ {3 2} M _ {3 2} - \lambda_ {1 8} M _ {1 8}
$$

$$
m _ {1 8} = \frac {1}{s + \lambda_ {1 8}} \left\{n _ {1 8} ^ {\circ} + 0. 3 6 \lambda_ {3 2} m _ {3 2} \right\}
$$

$$
s _ {3 2} - s _ {3 2} ^ {\circ} = \lambda_ {0 8} u _ {0 8} - \lambda_ {3 2} u _ {3 2}
$$

$$
n _ {3 2} = \frac {1}{s + \lambda_ {3 2}} \left\{n _ {3 2} ^ {\circ} + \lambda_ {0 8} n _ {0 8} \right\}
$$

$$
s \eta_ {0 8} - \eta_ {0 8} ^ {\circ} = \lambda_ {2 2} \eta_ {2 2} - \lambda_ {0 8} \eta_ {0 8}
$$

$$
n _ {0 8} = \frac {1}{s + \lambda_ {0 8}} \left\{n _ {0 8} ^ {\circ} + \lambda_ {2 2} n _ {2 2} \right\}
$$

$$
s n _ {2 2} - n _ {2 2} ^ {0} = - \lambda_ {2 2} n _ {2 2}
$$

$$
m _ {2 2} = \frac {1}{s + \lambda_ {2 2}} m _ {2 2} ^ {0}
$$

$$
\begin{array}{l} \therefore M _ {1 8} = \frac {1}{S + \lambda_ {1 8}} \left\{u _ {1 8} ^ {\circ} - \frac {0 . 3 6 \lambda_ {3 2}}{S + \lambda_ {3 2}} \left[ u _ {3 2} ^ {\circ} + \frac {\lambda_ {0 8}}{S + \lambda_ {0 8}} \left(u _ {0 8} ^ {\circ} \right. \right. \right. \\ + \frac {\lambda_ {2 2} \eta_ {2 2} ^ {o}}{s}) ] \rbrace \\ = \frac {u _ {1 9} ^ {\circ}}{s + \lambda_ {1 8}} + \frac {0 . 3 6 \lambda_ {3 2} u _ {3 2} ^ {\circ}}{(s + \lambda_ {1 8}) (s + \lambda_ {3 2})} \\ + \frac {0 . 3 6 \lambda_ {3 2} \lambda_ {0 8} u _ {0 8} ^ {o}}{(s + \lambda_ {1 8}) (s + \lambda_ {3 2}) (s + \lambda_ {0 8})} + \frac {0 . 3 6 \lambda_ {3 2} \lambda_ {0 8} \lambda_ {2 2} u _ {7 2} ^ {o}}{3 (s + \lambda_ {1 8}) (s + \lambda_ {3 2}) (s + \lambda_ {0 8})} \\ \end{array}
$$

There are an infinite set of possible situations and corresponding solutions associated with different reprocessing schemes. If most direct interest is the situation where

$$
n _ {1 8} ^ {\circ} = n _ {3 2} ^ {\circ} = n _ {0 8} ^ {\circ} = 0
$$

Then

$$
\begin{array}{l} S \equiv \lambda_ {1 8} N _ {1 8} = 0. 3 6 \lambda_ {3 2} \lambda_ {0 8} \lambda_ {\pi 2} \lambda_ {1 8} \left(\frac {N _ {2 2} ^ {0}}{N _ {2 3}}\right) \cdot N _ {2 3} \\ \left[ \frac {1}{\lambda_ {1 8} \lambda_ {3 2} \lambda_ {0 8}} - \left\{F _ {1} (\lambda_ {1 8}, \lambda_ {3 2}, \lambda_ {0 8}) + F _ {2} (\lambda_ {3 2}, \lambda_ {1 8}, \lambda_ {0 8}) + F _ {3} (\lambda_ {0 8}, \lambda_ {3 2}, \lambda_ {1 8}) \right\} \right] \\ \end{array}
$$

where

$$
F (a, b, c) = e ^ {- a t} / (b - a) (c - a) a
$$

S = emission rate for 2.51-keV 203 Tl gannas

and the units of $\lambda_{18}N_{18}$ are $^{208}\mathrm{TL}$ -decays/s. Substituting in $\mathbf{N}_{23} = 19\mathrm{gm}^{233}\mathrm{U}$ metal $\mathrm{cm}^{-3}\times 6.02\times 10^{23}233\mathrm{U}$ atoms per $^{233}_{S}233_{U}$ and the $\{\lambda_i\}$ , one gets a source rate

$$
\begin{array}{l} S = 1 9. x \frac {6 . 0 2 x 1 0 ^ {2 3}}{2 3 3} x 0. 3 6 x 1. 9 x 1 0 ^ {- 4} x 1. 2 x 1 0 ^ {- 8} x 3. 1 x 1 0 ^ {- 1 0} \\ \times 3. 9 \times 1 0 ^ {- 3} \left[ \frac {N _ {2 2}}{N _ {2 3}} \right] \left\{\frac {1}{\lambda_ {1 8} \lambda_ {3 2} \lambda_ {0 8}} - \sum_ {i = 1} ^ {3} F _ {i} \right\} \\ \end{array}
$$

$$
S \left(\frac {d}{s c m ^ {3}}\right) = 0. 0 4 9 \left[ \frac {N _ {2 2}}{N _ {2 3}} \right] \left\{\frac {1}{\lambda_ {1 8} \lambda_ {3 2} \lambda_ {D 8}} - \sum_ {i = 1} ^ {3} F _ {i} \right\}
$$

Note that for at least one $\mathbf{F}_i$ the exponential numerator should be near one and its denominator very small so that $\sum F_i$ is large. Section 3.5.11.2 evaluates S for situations of interest.

# 3.5.5 Pertinent Physics Metrics

3.5.5.1 BG: actual breeding gain. The conventional definition of BG is

$$
B G = \frac {\text {r a t e o f f i s s i l e p r o d u c t i o n}}{\text {r a t e o f f i s s i l e d e s t r u c t i o n}} \tag {3.5.5-1}
$$

The question then arises: which materials are fissile and which fertile? Convention says $^{234}\mathrm{U}$ is fertile, but in the very hard HCFR neutron spectrum it behaves like a fissile: $\mathcal{S}_{\mathrm{f}}$ exceeds $\mathcal{S}_{\mathrm{Y}}$ over most of the neutron spectrum (Fig. 2.1-5), suggesting a bare critical mass smaller than that for $^{237}\mathrm{Np}$ ( $69\mathrm{kg}$ ), $^{240}\mathrm{Tu}$ ( $159\mathrm{kg}$ ), or $^{241}\mathrm{Am}$ ( $114\mathrm{kg}$ ) [135]. $^{232}\mathrm{Th}$ and $^{238}\mathrm{U}$ cannot sustain a chain reaction. Thus we label $^{233}\mathrm{U}$ , $^{234}\mathrm{U}$ , and $^{235}\mathrm{U}$ as fissiles in an HCFR, $^{232}\mathrm{Th}$ and $^{238}\mathrm{U}$ as non-fissils.

BG can mean the instantaneous BG at any given point in time (of the burnup cycle) or a time-averaged BG such as over a given LFER re-fuelling cycle or reactor life. As the survey nature of this study precludes a detailed fuel management analysis, this study deals only with instantaneous BG, presumably on equilibrium cycle:

$$
\mathrm {B G} = \frac {\text {T h c a p t u r e r a t e}}{\text {r a t e o f} ^ {2 3 3} \mathrm {U} , ^ {2 3 4} \mathrm {U} , ^ {2 3 5} \mathrm {U} \text {f i s s i o n}}
$$

This certainly differs in $^{234}\mathrm{U}$ treatment from that for a thermal $\mathrm{Th}/^{233}\mathrm{U}-$ MSBR: however each reflects the physical realities of the corresponding neutron spectra, and this definition does agree with equation (3.5.5.-1!) above.

# 3.5.5.2 BGX: BG extended to zero neutron leakage. BGX

gauges the BG potential of a reactor design in the event that neutrons which presently escape from the reactor (outer) blanket (CB) were instead captured there in the same proportions as present.

$$
\mathrm {B G X} = (1 + \mathrm {B G}) \left\{1 + \left[ \frac {\text {T h c a p t u r e}}{\text {t o t a l a b s r a t e}} \right] _ {\mathrm {O B}} f _ {1 k g} \right\} - 1
$$

This metric thus indicates what additional BG can be obtained through a larger blanket.

# 3.5.5.3 BGP: BG potential associated with the core spectrum.

The theoretical limit to BG forms another valuable metric of an MCFR(Th). Taube calculated previously-unheard-of BG = 0.7-0.8 (BR = 1.7-1.8) for an MCFR(Pu).

Section 3.1.1.1 defined BG potential as the number of neutrons available for breeding per $^{233}\mathrm{U}$ fission. That ignored all actinides except $^{232}\mathrm{Th}$ and $^{233}\mathrm{U}$ . The net result depended primarily on $\gamma \sigma_{\mathrm{f}}$ for $^{233}\mathrm{U}$ and indicated a potential for BGs up to 0.4 on the $^{232}\mathrm{Th}/^{233}\mathrm{U}$ fuel cycle.

The ideal situation would be for $^{232}$ Th to capture neutrons which leave the core: i.e. for none to leak out the blanket nor be lost to parasitic capture. Then BG = $\gamma - 2$ . However, for the MCFR here we must return from utopia and allow at least for parasitic losses due to necessary core salt components. This then gives

$$
B C P = (\overline {{\nu}} - 2) _ {\text {r e a c t o r}} \quad - \quad \frac {\sum_ {\text {c o r e}} (\text {p a r a s i t i c c a p t u r e s})}{\sum_ {\text {r e a c t o r}} (\text {f i s s i o n s})}
$$

3.5.5.4 $\phi_{\text {core }}$ The core-averaged total flux will primarily indicate the rate of damage to, and thereby replacement of, the graphite tubes.

3.5.5.5 E core . The median core flux energy is found as- suming a constant lethargy flux within a group's boundaries. This metric proves to be a valuable barometer of BGP (Fig. 3.5-6) since $\overline{\nu}$ and $\sigma_{\mathrm{f}}$ increase with fast neutron energy while $\mathfrak{G}$ decreases.

3.5.5.6 Power density. Together with $\langle \mathbf{E} \rangle_{\text {core }} \varnothing$ , this metric determines $\varnothing_{\text {core }}$ . It also affects heat exchanger design: the higher the power density in the core fluid salt, the faster must heat per unit fluid volume be removed in the exchanger.

3.5.5.7 $\Phi$ (vessel), total and $>100\mathrm{keV}$ . Unlike the core tubes, the MCFR design and operation plan does not readily tolerate the replacement of the reactor vessel. Therefore the neutron damage, especially measured by the flux above $100\mathrm{keV}$ , should not exceed $10^{13}$ n cm $^{-2}$ sec $^{-1}$ or so.

![](images/e4480803f39cdca92287f85fd0dd1b2f4fcdb8ded9c6dacccbb7fd263daedf6f.jpg)  
Figure 3.5-6. Variation of Utopian BG (Ignoring Parasitic Losses and Leakage) with Core Flux-Spectrum Hardness.

3.5.5.8 Fuel inventory and doubling time. Doubling time (DT) provides a pertinent measure of the economics and physics of a breeder reactor: the time required to breed enough extra fuel to start up another reactor. That should really encompass the whole fuel cycle: core + blanket + primary loop + in-plant stockpiles + transport + fuel fabrication + fuel reprocessing + out-of-plant storage.

However, perhaps because solid fuel reactors all require similar ex-reactor fuel cycles, DT conventionally measures just the time for duplicating core and blanket. To facilitate comparison, this study adopts that definition also except that the MCFR must also include its full primary circuit contents:

$$
\mathrm {D T} = \frac {\mathrm {U} _ {\mathrm {i}} + \mathrm {U} _ {\mathrm {o}} + \mathrm {U} _ {\mathrm {b}}}{\text {B G p r o d u c t i o n r a t e}} \tag {3.5.5-2}
$$

Here

$U_{i} = U$ content inside the reactor vessel; more precisely between the ex-vessel bends of the tubes (Fig. 3.2-9, -10, and -11)

$U_{0} = U$ inventory in primary circuit beyond (outside) the reactor vessel: piping, pumps, and heat exchangers. Section 3.4 indicates the volume $V_{0} = 2 - 3\mathrm{m}^{3}$ .

$U_{b} =$ Blanket U content

This should result in a conservative comparison of the MCFR fuel cycle to the solid fuel cycle of other breeder reactors: solid fuel will require much more inventory in storage, transportation, decladding, and fabrication operations; the MCFR, little. Both cycles should involve similar amounts of chemical processing, but

the MCFR going from liquid to liquid state, may require fewer steps and therefore less U.

The odd geometry of the skewed tube configuration increases the in-vessel U content, $\mathsf{U}_{\mathsf{i}}$ , considerably beyond the calculated critical spherical mass, $\mathsf{U}_{\mathsf{s}}$ . If there was only one cylindrical tube (N=1) then the volume $\mathsf{V}_{\mathsf{i}}$ associated with $\mathsf{U}_{\mathsf{i}}$ would be

$$
V _ {i} (N = 1) = \pi D ^ {2} L / 4 = V _ {s} / S F (L / D) \tag {3.5.5-3}
$$

where

$$
\begin{array}{l} V _ {S} = \text {c r i t i c a l v o l u m e i n s p h e r i s c a l g e o m e t r y} \\ S F = L / D \text {d e p e n d e n t s h a p e f a c t o r} (\text {S e c . 3 . 5 . 1 . 2}) \\ L = \text {c r i t i c a l} L = H \\ H = \text {d i s t a n c e} \\ \text {m e t e r} \quad \mathrm {b l a n k e t} \quad \text {s u r r o n d i n g} \quad \text {c o r e , w e} \quad \mathrm {e s t i m a t e} \quad \mathrm {H} = 5 \mathrm {m} \\ D = \text {c r i t i c a l} \\ \end{array}
$$

Rearranging Eq. 3.5.5-3, get

$$
L / D = \left(\pi L ^ {3} S F (L / D) / 4 V _ {S}\right) ^ {\frac {1}{2}} \tag {3.5.5-4}
$$

Since SF depends on L/D, Eq. 3.5.5-4 is transcendental: it must be solved iteratively or by graphical intersection technique.

If $N > 1$ and the tubes lay sufficiently apart from one another (loosely coupled), then $V_{i} (N > 1) \approx N V_{i} (N = 1)$ . However, this is not of interest because we prefer strong intertube neutronic coupling (Section 3.1.3.2).

With strong coupling, if N tubes were not skewed but lay ad

jacent to one another, then their combined cross sectional area would approach that of a single large tube, then

$$
V _ {i} (N > 1) = V _ {i} (N = 1) \text {o f E q .} 3. 5. 5 - 3.
$$

In contrast, when skewed the maximum, the tubes closely approach one another only near their midpoints. Then no tube sustains the neutron chain reaction very well outside the close-approach zone. The critical geometry of that zone should resemble a square cylinder $(\mathsf{L} / \mathsf{D} = 1)$ , somewhat squeezed at its middle into the shape of an hour glass or a wheat stack ().

The large mean free paths of fast neutrons tend to negate the "tubular" protusions of core salt from this geometry: next collisions will probably occur in the neutron-absorbing blanket. Then with $L / D \approx 1$

$$
V _ {S} / S F (L / D = 1) = N T D _ {t} ^ {2} \cdot D _ {t} / 4
$$

where

$$
D _ {t} = d i a m e t e r o f a s i n g l e t u b e
$$

$$
D _ {t} = \left(4 V _ {s} / N \pi S F (L / D = 1)\right) ^ {1 / 3}
$$

$$
\begin{array}{l} V _ {i} (N > 1) = N \pi H D _ {t} ^ {2} / 4 = H ((V _ {s} / 0. 9 7) ^ {2} N \pi / 4) ^ {1 / 3} \\ = 0. 9 4 \mathrm {H} (\mathrm {N V} _ {\mathrm {s}} ^ {2}) ^ {1 / 3} \tag {3.5.5-5} \\ \end{array}
$$

Figure 3.5-7 displays the results of Eq. 3.5.5-3 and 3.5.5-5. The differences in $V_{i}$ correspond to the differences between zero and maximum skew angle respectively. Thus the true results would lie intermediate. For the present study we take $V_{i}$ at some value of low significant figure slightly below the Eq. 3.5-7 line (for $N = 3$ ). This should result in a conservative estimate of DT. To get $U_{1}$ or $U_{0}$ we multiply $V_{i}$ or $V_{0}$ by $U_{s} / V_{s}$ .

![](images/f0d80bd95d9b1eaca5fa5c9964415bf220139a963b08d0a101a4da1d7ce43860.jpg)  
V（m）  
Figure 3.5-7. Variation of In-Vessel Primary-Circuit Volume with Critical-Sphere Volume for Maximum- and Zero-Angle Tube Skew

# 3.5.6 Fuel Cycle Modelling

3.5.6.1 Fuel cycle model. Figure 3.5-8 describes the actinide flow scheme. (Section 3.7 describes the chemical aspects of the fuel cycle.) Th supply to both primary circuit and blanket maintains the Th concentrations. U exits with fractional removal rates p and b respectively, passing into the shim tank. b is actually a net removal rate: part of the removed blanket U returns.

![](images/4bd4c5eb1008324263b431af1d9f536299704461be681e3e89a48879948d17f2.jpg)  
Fig. 3.5-8. Actinide Flow Scheme

Fraction $f$ of the shim tank inflow feeds the primary circuit, simultaneous with $pU_p$ and fission product removal. The remaining fraction $g = 1 - f$ is exported as BG.

Extending Sec. 3.5.4.1, U varies in the blanket according to

$$
d U _ {3 b} / d t = \left\langle \sigma_ {T h x} \phi \right\rangle_ {b} T h _ {b} - \left\langle \sigma_ {3 a} \phi \right\rangle_ {b} U _ {3 b} - b U _ {3 b} \tag {3.5.6-1}
$$

$$
d U _ {4 b} / d t = \left\langle \sigma_ {3 r} \phi \right\rangle_ {b} U _ {3 b} - \left\langle \sigma_ {4 r} \phi \right\rangle_ {b} U _ {4 b} - b U _ {4 b} \tag {3.5.6-2}
$$

$$
d U _ {5 b} / d t = \left\langle \sigma_ {4 r} \phi \right\rangle_ {b} U _ {4 b} - \left\langle \sigma_ {s a} \phi \right\rangle_ {b} U _ {5 b} - b U _ {5 b} \tag {3.5.6-3}
$$

Similarly in the primary circuit

$$
\begin{array}{l} \mathrm {U} _ {3 p} / \mathrm {d t} = \left\langle \mathrm {S} _ {\text {o r}} \phi \right\rangle_ {s} \mathrm {T h} _ {s} - \left\langle \mathrm {S} _ {3 a} \phi \right\rangle_ {s} \mathrm {U} _ {3 s} - \mathrm {p U} _ {3 p} \\ + f \left(p U _ {3 p} + b U _ {3 b}\right) \tag {3.5.6-4} \\ \end{array}
$$

$$
\begin{array}{l} d U _ {4 p} / d t = \left\langle \sigma_ {3 r} \phi \right\rangle_ {s} U _ {3 s} - \left\langle \sigma_ {4 c} \phi \right\rangle_ {s} U _ {4 s} - p U _ {4 p} \\ + r \left(p U _ {4 p} + b U _ {4 t}\right) \tag {3.5.6-5} \\ \end{array}
$$

$$
\begin{array}{l} d U _ {5 p} / d t = \left\langle \sigma_ {4 r} \phi \right\rangle_ {s} U _ {4 s} - \left\langle \sigma_ {5 4} \phi \right\rangle_ {s} U _ {5 s} - p U _ {5 p} \\ + f \left(p U _ {5 p} + b U _ {5 b}\right) \tag {3.5.6-6} \\ \end{array}
$$

Here the subscript $s$ denotes the spherical core region. The integrated reaction rates may differ slightly in few-tube geometry, but they can be adjusted later to reaction rates from assemblies of higher Th concentration.

We define here

$$
\begin{array}{l} U _ {s} = U _ {3 s} + U _ {4 s} + U _ {5 s} (3.5.6-7) \\ U _ {b} = U _ {3 b} + U _ {4 b} + U _ {5 b} (3.5.6-8) \\ U _ {i p} = U _ {i s} V _ {p} / V _ {s}, i = 3, 4, \text {o r} 5 \text {f o r} ^ {2 3 3} U ^ {2 3 4} U, \text {o r} ^ {2 3 5} U. \\ \zeta = v _ {p} / v _ {s} \\ V _ {p} = \text {V o l u m e i n t h e p r i m a r y c i r c u i t} \\ V _ {s} = \text {V o l u m e o f a c r i t i c a l s p h e r e} \\ \end{array}
$$

Then, substituting in Eq. 3.5.6-4 through -6 for the $\left\{\mathsf{U}_{\mathsf{ip}}\right\} -$

$$
\begin{array}{l} \frac {d U _ {3 s}}{d t} = \left\langle \sigma_ {a v} \phi \right\rangle_ {s} T h _ {s} - \left\langle \sigma_ {3 a} \phi \right\rangle_ {s} U _ {3 s} - p 5 U _ {3 s} + f (p 5 U _ {3 s} + b U _ {3 b}) \\ \frac {d U _ {4 s}}{d t} = \left\langle \sigma_ {3 r} \phi \right\rangle_ {s} U _ {3 s} - \left\langle \sigma_ {4 a} \phi \right\rangle_ {s} U _ {4 s} - p \zeta U _ {4 s} + f (p \zeta U _ {4 s} + b U _ {4 b}) \\ \left. \frac {d U _ {5 s}}{d t} = \left\langle \sigma_ {4 r} \phi \right\rangle_ {s} U _ {4 s} - \left\langle \sigma_ {5 a} \phi \right\rangle_ {s} U _ {5 s} - p S U _ {5 s} + f (\bar {r} S U _ {5 s} + b U _ {5 0}) \right. \\ \end{array}
$$

3.5.6.2 Equilibrium fuel cycle. We now study a specific situation of interest - the equilibrium cycle where compositions have stabilized and $\left\{\frac{dJ_{ij}}{dt}\right\}$ each $= 0$ . The starter fuel does not affect the equilibrium composition; only the feed material and neutron spectrum control it. This section seeks to determine the relations which express

1. The equilibrium proportions of actinides in primary circuit and blanket   
2. The equilibrium breeding gain and/or reactor doubling time.

ANISN calculations provide the reaction probability rates (per atom) $\langle \sigma \phi \rangle$ . In addition we specify the core uranium concentration, and blanket thickness. Together with a critical core radius search, this fixes $U_{s}, Th_{s}$ , and $Th_{b}$ . Nine unknowns remain: $U_{3s}, U_{4s}, U_{5s}, U_{3b}, U_{4b}, U_{5b}, f, b$ , and c. There are seven equations, 3.5.6-1 - 3.5.6-7. That suggests that we also choose the reprocessing rates b and c; these will then determine f, and from it, the BG export and doubling time.

Now setting $\mathrm{d}U_{ij} / \mathrm{d}t = 0$ for each $i$ and $j$ , then from Eq. 3.5.6-1 through 3.5.6-3, in the blanket:

$$
U _ {3 b} = \frac {\left\langle \sigma_ {\pi r} \phi \right\rangle_ {b}}{b + \left\langle \sigma_ {3 a} \phi \right\rangle_ {b}} \quad T h _ {b} \tag {3.5.6-9}
$$

$$
U _ {4 b} = \frac {\left\langle \sigma_ {3 x} \phi \right\rangle_ {b}}{b + \left\langle \sigma_ {4 a} \phi \right\rangle_ {b}} U _ {3 b} \tag {3.5.6-10}
$$

$$
U _ {\overline {{2}} b} = \frac {\langle \sigma_ {4 y} \phi \rangle_ {b}}{b + \langle \sigma_ {5 a} \phi \rangle_ {b}} U _ {4 b} \tag {3.5.6-11}
$$

Normalizing uranium contents to $\mathbf{Th}_0$ , get

$$
u _ {3 b} = \frac {\left\langle \sigma_ {n r} \phi \right\rangle_ {b}}{b + \left\langle \sigma_ {3 a} \phi \right\rangle_ {b}} \quad \left(^ {2 3 3} U _ {b} a t o m s / ^ {2 3 2} T h _ {b} a t o m\right)
$$

$$
u _ {4 b} = \frac {\langle \sigma_ {3 r} \phi \rangle_ {b}}{b + \langle \sigma_ {4 s} \phi \rangle_ {b}} u _ {3 b}
$$

$$
u _ {5 b} = \frac {\langle \sigma_ {4 r} \phi \rangle_ {b}}{b + \langle \sigma_ {5 a} \phi \rangle_ {b}} u _ {4 b}
$$

where

$$
u _ {i b} = U _ {i b} / \mathrm {T h} _ {b}
$$

In the core, by equations 3.5.6-4 through 3.5.6-6, get

$$
U _ {3 s} = \frac {\left\langle C _ {t h r} 4 \right\rangle_ {s} \cdot \operatorname {T h} _ {s} + b f U _ {3 b}}{\left\langle C _ {3 a} \phi \right\rangle_ {s} + p 5 (1 - f)} \tag {3.5.6-12}
$$

$$
U _ {4 S} = \frac {\left\langle \sigma_ {3 r} \phi \right\rangle_ {S} U _ {3 S} + b f U _ {4 b}}{\left\langle a _ {4 d} \phi \right\rangle_ {S} + p S (1 - f)} \tag {3.5.6-13}
$$

$$
U _ {5 3} = \frac {\langle \sigma_ {4 x} \phi \rangle_ {s} U _ {4 s} + b f U _ {5 b}}{\langle \sigma_ {s a} \phi \rangle_ {s} + p \text {与} (1 - r)} \tag {3.5.6-14}
$$

Normalizing to $U_{s}$ and setting $c = p\zeta$ :

$$
\begin{array}{l} u _ {3 s} = \frac {\langle \sigma_ {n r} \phi \rangle_ {s} t _ {s} + n u _ {3 b}}{\langle \sigma_ {3 a} \phi \rangle_ {s} + c (1 - f)} \\ u _ {4 s} = \frac {\langle \sigma_ {3 r} \phi \rangle_ {s} u _ {3 s} + n u _ {4 b}}{\langle \sigma_ {4 a} \phi \rangle_ {s} + c (1 - f)} \\ u _ {5 s} = \frac {\langle \sigma_ {4 r} \phi \rangle_ {s} u _ {4 s} + n u _ {5 b}}{\langle \sigma_ {5 a} \phi \rangle_ {s} + c (1 - f)} \\ \end{array}
$$

Here

$$
\begin{array}{l} u _ {i s} = U _ {i s} / U _ {s} \\ t _ {s} = \mathrm {T h} _ {s} / U _ {s} \\ n = b f - t _ {b} \\ t _ {b} = T h _ {b} / U _ {s} \\ \end{array}
$$

Now to calculate these requires knowledge of f. Summing Eq.

3.5.6-5 through 3.5.6-7 gives a recursion formula suitably weighted over all the actinide contents:

$$
f + \frac {c U _ {s} + \sum_ {i = 3} ^ {4} \left\langle \sigma_ {i f} \phi \right\rangle_ {s} \cdot U _ {i s} + \left\langle \sigma_ {s a} \phi \right\rangle_ {s} U _ {s s} - \left\langle \sigma_ {s r} \phi \right\rangle_ {s} T h _ {s}}{c U _ {s} + b U _ {b}}
$$

Normalizing numerator and denominator to $U_{s}$ , get

$$
f = \frac {c + \sum_ {i = 3} ^ {4 i} \left\langle \sigma_ {i r} \phi \right\rangle_ {s} u _ {i s} + \left\langle \sigma_ {s a} \phi \right\rangle_ {s} u _ {s s} - \left\langle \sigma_ {m r} \phi \right\rangle_ {s} t _ {s}}{c + b \cdot u _ {b} \cdot t _ {b}} \tag {3.5.6-15}
$$

Our iterative procedure will be to

(1) Choose b   
(2) Calculate $\left\{u_{ib}\right\}$ and $u_{b}$   
(3) Make initial $\left\{\mathbf{u}_{\mathbf{i}\mathbf{s}}\right\}$ guess

(4) Choose c   
(5) Calculate $f$ by Eq. 3.5.6-15   
(6) Calculate corresponding $\left\{\mathbf{u}_{\mathbf{i}\mathbf{s}}\right\}$ and $\mathbf{u}_s = \sum \mathbf{u}_{\mathbf{i}\mathbf{s}}$   
(7) If $|u_s - 1| < \epsilon$ , renormalize $\{u_{is}\}$ and $u_s$   
(8) Reset starter guess and go to (4) until desired range of c is covered.   
(9) Return to (1) until desired range of $b$ is covered.

The BG export rate is

$$
\dot {U} _ {e} = (1 - f) \left(c U _ {s} + b U _ {b}\right) \tag {3.5.6-16}
$$

The doubling time with respect to reactor inventory is then

$$
\mathrm {D T} = \frac {\mathrm {U} _ {\mathrm {t}}}{(1 - \mathrm {f}) (\mathrm {c U} _ {\mathrm {s}} + \mathrm {b U} _ {\mathrm {b}})} \tag {3.5.6-17}
$$

where

$$
U _ {t} = U _ {i} + U _ {o} + U _ {b} (\text {S e c . 3 . 5 . 5 . 8}),
$$

ANISN calculates $\mathbf{U}_{\mathbf{s}}$ and reaction rates (Table 3.5-VI),

$U_{b}$ depends on removal rate $b$ (Eq. 3.5.6-9; 10 & 11)

Fig. 3.5-7 provides the means of getting $U_{i}$ from $U_{s}$

$U_{0} = 2 - 3\pi^{3}$ , as discussed in Sec. 3.5.5.8.

3.5.6.3 On the choice of U removal rates c and b. This section studies how c and b affect the core $^{233}\mathrm{U} / ^{234}\mathrm{U}$ ratio and blanket U content ( $\mathsf{U}_{\mathsf{b}}$ ) using reaction rates calculated for an MCFR model with 30/35/35 NaCl/ThCl $_4$ /UCl $_3$ molar composition and 7/1 $^{233}\mathrm{U} / ^{234}\mathrm{U}$ ratio in the core salt. Section 3.5.8 shows the importance of high $^{233}\mathrm{U} / ^{234}\mathrm{U}$ ratio in the core salt. $\mathsf{U}_{\mathsf{b}}$ should be minimized because

it degrades BG export $(\dot{U}_{e})$ and increases doubling time (DT); it also generates unwanted power and radioactivity in the blanket.

Figure 3.5-9 shows how increasing $b$ quickly reduces $U_b$ to nil. The total $U$ inventory $(U_t)$ then comprises just the primary circuit contents, $U_i + U_o$ . Meanwhile, $\dot{U}_e$ rises from oblivion to its maximum level. Since $U_t$ and $U_e$ vary with $b$ in opposite directions, their quotient DT (Eq. 3.5.6-17) varies even more strongly with $b$ (Fig. 3.5-10). Core $^{233}U/^{234}U$ ratio also varies markedly with $b$ in Fig. 3.5-10.

All these comparisons argue for $b \geq 10^{-7}$ , but not much beyond that. Fig. 3.5-11 shows that high c keeps $^{233}\mathrm{U} / ^{234}\mathrm{U}$ high and only slightly affects DT adversely. It seems to warrant c as high as $10^{-6} / \mathrm{s}$ , but not crucially so. Note that $c = 10^{-7}$ implies a primary $\mathrm{U_p}$ removal rate of $p = c / s = c V_s / V_p$ . Thus the requirement on p will typically fall a factor of 10 or more below (be far less stringent than) that on c.

A rate of $10^{-6} / \mathrm{s}$ implies the removal of 1 ppm of the U per second, or a complete processing of that zone every 11.6 days. Though that may be doable, a rate of $10^{-7} / \mathrm{s}$ or full processing in 116 days seems more reasonable while imposing no severe penalty.

Detailed analysis in other studies shows the same b and c behavior patterns there. Thus we adopt $b = c = 10^{-7} / s$ as a reference for this work. We also conclude that physical reprocessing limitations should not prevent us from achieving near-optimum $233_{\mathrm{U}} / 234_{\mathrm{U}}$ ratio and DT and small $U_{b}$ .

![](images/b935ee0c589f37b944a895d5149d15f1fb4250f9842556435eda56addfa3b9d7.jpg)  
Figure 3.5-9. Sensitivity of Blanket Uranium Content $(U_{b})$ to Its Extraction Rate (b)

Basis: ANISN calculations of critical size and reaction rates of a core mixture with 30 mole % NaCl, 35 mole % ThCl₄, 35 mole % UCl₃,²³₃U/²³₄U ratio of 7.

3.5.7.1 Inner blanket study. Taube studied [17] inner blankets up to $1.1\mathrm{m}$ in radius for spherical MCFR(Pu) and (fluoride) MFFR(Pu). In each case the outer blanket remained one-meter thick. With pure fissile (no fertile) salt in the core, the BG slightly increased for an MCFR(PU) and decreased for an MHTR(Pu). Fig. 3.5-12 shows the changes by zone in the MFFR(Pu) study: inner blanket breeding increases with its volume, but also softens the core neutron spectrum. The result is increased core BG but lower total BG, more so for F than Cl salt. Thus for a

![](images/d269a4fef2b17a85f2a7464d5abdba18eff0e1070ff290544c2e1cad34ff26d8.jpg)  
Fig. 3.5-12 Impact of Inner Blanket Volume on BG for a MFFR(Pu)

fixed reactor diameter, which controls the overall plant size, an inner blanket does not appear worthwhile relative to just increasing the outer blanket thickness. Furthermore even if marginal BG advantages were found, the added engineering complexity would probably discourage it.

To verify the effect of an inner blanket a study was made in spherical geometry. Figures 3.5-13 and 3.5-14 show the results for a constant total blanket thickness of $200\mathrm{cm}$ . As the IB diameter increases, the annular core moves radially outward. As a result

1. The core volume increases, decreasing the power density   
2. The core spectrum softens   
3. Neutron leakage from the reactor increases   
4. Inner breeding increases and outer breeding decreases resulting in a strong net decrease

The accelerating BG decrease is primarily caused by the increasing neutron leakage and secondly by the softened core spectrum effect on BG potential.

3.5.7.2 Optimum outer blanket thickness. A study was made to determine what constituted an infinite (outer) blanket thickness to BG. It assumed no inner blanket. The study considered $\mathrm{ThCl}_4 / \mathrm{UCl}_3$ molar ratios in the core of 0,1, and 3. The NaCl molar content in the fuel salt was $30\%$ . The results (Fig. 3.5-15) indicate that 2 m is near infinite.

Figure 3.5-16 shows that the outer blanket also shields the vessel from the high core flux: a two-meter thickness reduces the damage ( $>0.1 \, \text{MeV}$ ) flux by over 3 magnitudes down to the level of the delayed neutrons.

Median Core Flux Energy (MeV)

BG

Bases: 30/35/35 molar proportions of Na/ThCl4/UCl3 in the core 200 cm total (inner + outer) blanket thickness Ni reflector

3.5.7.3 Reflector. A pair of ANISN calculations determined the effect of reducing the reflector from 40 cm graphite to 20 cm using a keff-precision of 0.001 or $0.1\%$ , P1 cross sections, and S8 quadrature. With a 2 m blanket, the BG dropped from 0.3446

to 0.3442 or $0.2\%$ . The critical radius increased from 26.11 to 26.14 cm or $0.1\%$ (a critical volume increase of $0.3\%$ ). Thus cutting the reflector thickness in half barely shows up above the accuracy of the calculations. This result corresponds well to that of the previous section: namely that two meters constitutes a near-infinite blanket thickness.

Comparison of C and Ni reflectors for a 3/1 Th/U core ratio and 5-m thick blanket showed no detectable differences. For a 0/1 Th/U core ratio and 1-m thick blanket, C performed much better (BG=0.106) than Ni (BC=0.040); the critical core radius differed little. The BG difference probably stems from the moderating power of C: neutrons reflected by C stand a much better chance of capture by Th than those reflected by Ni. This recommends C for cases where a non-infinite blanket is used. For ≥ 1.5-2.0 m thick blankets and 65/35 ThCl₄/NaCl mixture, a neutron reflector appears unneeded.

3.5.7.4 Core tube material. In an MCFR (Pu) study [75], a 20 Mo/80 Fe alloy for the core/blanket wall reduced BG by 0.04 relative to a C wall. As the $20\%$ Ho dominated the neutron absorptions there $(61\%)$ , $100\%$ Mo tubes might reduce BG by 0.2. The MCFR(Pu) study assumed in-core cooling with the tubes occupying $5.9\%$ of the core volume. In the present MCFR(Th) concept the 2-cm tubes occupy several times that proportion of volume but they lie more on the periphery. Thus a BG effect similar to 0.2 might be expected.

3.5.8 Reactor Design: Controlling the Core $^{233}\mathrm{U} / ^{234}\mathrm{U}$ Ratio

3.5.8.1 Basis for studying the ratio. $^{234}\mathrm{U}$ content affects the Section 2-adduced goals: hard neutron spectrum, high BG, and minimum presence of Pu and other heavy actinides. Although $^{234}\mathrm{U}$ content is not a design (independent) variable but rather a physics consequence (dependent variable), still design variables like NaCl/ UCl₃/ThCl₄ molar composition can influence it. Therefore we study its effects as if it were an independent variable, looking at $^{233}\mathrm{U}/234\mathrm{U} = 3,5$ , and 7.

The MCFR model used as a basis in the study included a core salt molar composition of $30/70 / \mathrm{C}$ NaCl/UCl₃/ThCl₄ and a 200 cm blanket.

3.5.8.2 Effect of the ratio on spectrum, BG, and Fu presence.

Fig. 3.5-17 shows that $^{234}\mathrm{U}$ softens the neutron spectrum ( $\langle \mathbf{E} \rangle_{\text{core}}$ decreases. This is probably due to a higher $\langle \sigma_{\text{inel}} \rangle / \langle \sigma_{\text{fiss}} \rangle$ ratio for $^{234}\mathrm{U}$ compared to that for $^{233}\mathrm{U}$ . $^{234}\mathrm{U}$ also reduces BG and power density. Table 3.5-IX shows that the major BG reduction stems directly from the lower $\langle \nabla \sigma_{\mathrm{f}} - \sigma_{\mathrm{c}} \rangle$ value for $^{234}\mathrm{U}$ , not from spectral softening. This same phenomenon decreases the reactivity of high $^{234}\mathrm{U}$ systems: that accounts for the larger critical volumes and lower power densities.

Intuitively, a high ratio will minimize the presence of Pu and other heavy actinides (the third goal): $234$ U heads the chain that leads to them.

In summary, a high $^{233}\mathrm{U} / ^{234}\mathrm{U}$ ratio promotes all three goals of hard neutron spectrum, high BG, and minimal Pu presence.

![](images/6fedd36b6052e7e64243052249e6d1ae94aff1e325ff34bcd2a222d10bc0211c.jpg)  
Figure 3.5-17. Correlation of Decreasing $^{234}\mathrm{U}$ Content With Key Core Physics Parameters

Table 3.5-IX Calculation of Neutron Production by the Two Principal U Isotopes   

<table><tr><td rowspan="2">233U/234U Ratio</td><td colspan="3">value for &lt;ν σε - σa&gt;</td></tr><tr><td>233U</td><td>234U</td><td>Uavg</td></tr><tr><td>3</td><td>3.27</td><td>1.16</td><td>2.74</td></tr><tr><td>5</td><td>3.26</td><td>1.18</td><td>2.91</td></tr><tr><td>7</td><td>3.33</td><td>1.02</td><td>3.04</td></tr></table>

3.5.8.3 Effect on reactor fissile inventory and doubling time. Because $^{234}\mathrm{U}$ is a fissile MCFR fuel, the reactor U inventory varies little in this study. However, the U export rate (Eq. 3.5.6-16) does increase with decreasing $^{234}\mathrm{U}$ content, reaching 0.25 MT/yr for the ratio $^{233}\mathrm{U}/^{234}\mathrm{U} = 7$ . Figure 3.5-18 shows the consequent effect on doubling time. This recommends a high $^{233}\mathrm{U}/^{234}\mathrm{U}$ ratio; however, significant effort to reach higher ratios may not be warranted.

![](images/0343cd0c707ba4ff7039396122f02a72aa49b344aa0a975caa06d86c4803f0f4.jpg)  
Figure 3.5-18. Variation of Doubling Time with Core Salt $^{233}\mathrm{U} / ^{234}\mathrm{U}$ Ratio

Basis: 30/70/0 NaCl/UCl₃/ThCl₄ molar salt composition in the core, 200 cm blanket, C reflector; b = c = 10⁻⁷/s

3.5.8.4 Deducing the ratio on equilibrium cycle. Given a set of reaction rates and choosing b and c, the equations of Sec. 3.5.8.2 specify the $^{233}\mathrm{U} / ^{234}\mathrm{U}$ ratio. However, this ratio for the fuel cycle will depend on the reaction rates used. These, in turn, depend on the ratio assumed apriori in the neutronic analysis of the equilibrium fuel cycle. Figure 3.5-19 suggests that the two converge near $^{233}\mathrm{U} / ^{234}\mathrm{U} = 9\frac{1}{2}$ for $\mathrm{b=c=10^{-7}}$ and near $12\frac{1}{2}-13$ for $\mathrm{b=c=10^{-6}}$ . This is for a 30/70/0 NaCl/UCl₃/ThCl₄ molar composition. Similar behavior is presumed for other MCFR models.

The results of later calculations with the refined set of P1 Cl cross sections (Sec. 3.5.3.2) suggest a slightly lower value, nearer to $233_{\mathrm{U}} / 234_{\mathrm{U}} = 8 \frac{1}{2}$ and $11 \frac{1}{2}$ for $b = c = 10^{-7}$ and $10^{-6}$ , respectively.

Core $^{233}\mathrm{U} / ^{234}\mathrm{U}$ Ratio on Equilibrium Fuel Cycle Deduced from ANISN-Calculated Reaction Rates

![](images/35bffe8f0e164c92e70ac9fdda1b3f96f573c0bbdcd0b1a1defe0cb8ee458968.jpg)  
Core $^{233}\mathrm{U} / ^{234}\mathrm{U}$ Ratio Assumed in Calculating Reaction Rates with ANISN   
Figure 3.5-19. Calculational Approach to the Equilibrium Core $233_{\mathrm{U}} / 231_{\mathrm{U}}$ Ratio

Bases = 30/70/0 molar proportions of NaCl/ThCl4/UCl3 in the core, 200 cm blanket

# 3.5.9 Reactor Design: Choosing the Core Salt ThCl/UCl3 Ratio

To approach its BG potential (BGX or BGP) the reactor must capture its excess neutrons in fertile material. One means of reducing neutron leakage from the reactor is to thicken the outer blanket (Sec. 3.5.6.2) so that the fertile fuel captures or reflects most of the neutrons which escape the core. Another way is to add fertile material to the core fuel thereby reducing the core neutron leakage. However, this also softens the neutron spectrum, which lowers the BG potential (BGP).

With an MCFR(U/Pu) Taube reported [17] BG up to 0.7-0.8 with such techniques. The following subsections study the optimum fertile content in an MCFR(Th) core. They assume a salt mixture of $30\%$

NaCl and $70\%$ $(\mathrm{UCl}_3 + \mathrm{ThCl}_4)$ and a $^{233}\mathrm{U} / ^{234}\mathrm{U}$ ratio of 7/1.

3.5.9.1 Effect on BG. Fig. 3.5-20 shows the results of ANISN calculations. Neutrons leak significantly from a one-meter blanket so the choice of reflector affects BG. Then one prefers $\mathrm{ThCl}_4 / \mathrm{UCl}_3 = 0$ .

When the blanket thickness exceeds $2.0 \, \text{m}$ however, few neutrons leak and the reflector has little effect. [Th] = 0 then produces the greatest BG because it engenders the hardest neutron spectrum (Fig. 3.5-21) and consequently, the most excess neutrons (Sec. 3.5.5.5).

Fig. 3.5-20 also shows the BG from the 1956 ORNL study [13] of an MCFR(Pu) which included a Pb core-reflector and a graphite-moderated thin blanket. The larger $\sqrt{v}$ value for Pu causes the high BG. Few neutrons leak despite low fertile content, but this is because of high (83%) carrier salt content. BG could actually be higher were it not for the spectrum softening and neutron absorption by the carrier salt.

3.5.9.2 Effect on reactor fissile inventory and doubling times. Substituting $\mathrm{ThCl}_4$ for $\mathrm{UCl}_3$ decreases density. However, the critical core radius increases near proportionally, and volume increases even faster. Thus, overall, Th dilution increases the spherical critical mass $U_{s}$ (Fig. 3.5-22) and the corresponding mass in the tubes $U_{i}$ (from Fig. 3.5-7). In contrast, the primary circuit volume outside the vessel, $V_{o}$ , remains fixed, so $U_{o}$ decreases.

While $V_{o}$ exceeds $V_{i}$ , $U_{t} = U_{i} + U_{o} + U_{b}$ decreases. ( $U_{b}$ plays little role while $b > 10^{-7}$ .) Eventually the Th proportion reaches a level where the internal volume $V_{i}$ exceeds the fixed $V_{o}$ . Then $U_{t}$ begins

Breeding Gain

![](images/00d94eba8c30f2bf436c1b25e01a84fe94c695a3232915f9aaa2e568cc2dea4d.jpg)  
Figure 3.5-20. Effect of Fertile Presence in the Core Salt upon BG: Lowering of the BG Potential; Trapping of Leakage Neutrons

to increase with increasing Th/U ratio. This $U_{t}$ -minimum occurs near $\mathrm{ThCl}_4 / \mathrm{UCl}_3 = 1 - 2$ .

In the 1956 ORNL study [13] of an MCFR(Pu) the minimum plant inventory occurred at a $^{238}\mathrm{U} / \mathrm{Pu}$ ratio of 2 (Fig. 3.5-23), similar to this work. The MCFR(Pu) total fissile inventory however lies roughly a factor of 3 below that for the present MCFR(Th) study: the reason is great dilution of the Pu fuel mixture by carrier salt ( $83\%$ molar content).

In Fig. 3.5-24 the BG export rate $\dot{U}_{e}$ for $b = c = 10^{-7} / s$ (defined by Eq. 3.5.6-16) stays fairly constant as the core Th/U ratio changes. By Eq. 3.5.6-17 then, DT behavior must closely follow that of $U_{t}$ . Fig. 3.5-24 shows that the lowest DT clearly occurs at a $\mathrm{ThCl}_4 / \mathrm{UCl}_3$ ratio $\neq 0$ : somewhere around 1-2.

![](images/2c47ea456779801977bc0fb72d7f3e2a14a81e7e11ae56864609ffcadba67757.jpg)

# 3.5.7.3 Effect on power density and flux levels

The power density limit of 10 MJ/liter (Section 3.2.2.2) reflects more a premonition of problems than a recognition of any specific ones. Still, flux levels in the core tubes and the pressure vessel closely relate to power density (section 3.2.2.3). Figure 3.5-25 shows how $\mathrm{ThCl}_4$ dilution of the core salt markedly reduces power density and fluxes alike.

The skewed-tube geometry will further increase the critical mass (lower the power density). A cylinder with length-to-diameter ratio $(L / D)$ near 1.0 requires about $10\%$ more critical mass in low density metal systems than does a sphere; with $L / D \sim 2$ , about $40\%$ more. The heterogeneity of multiple skewed tubes further increases critical mass as does neutron capture and scatter by the blanket $\mathrm{ThCl}_4$ in between the tubes. Altogether these effects should reduce power density and fluxes by more than a factor of two compared to spheres. Applied to Fig. 3.5-25, the result is that $\mathrm{ThCl}_4 / \mathrm{UCl}_3 = 0.1$ now satisfies the power density criteria of $< 10 \mathrm{MW} / \mathrm{l}$ .

At higher $\mathrm{ThCl}_4 / \mathrm{UCl}_3$ ratios the $>100\mathrm{keV}$ flux in the vessel falls below $10^{13}\mathrm{ncm}^{-2}\mathrm{s}^{-1}$ or $< 10^{22}\mathrm{ncm}^{-2}$ in 30 years. That level will allow operation over the reactor life for many metals.

Meanwhile the core flux level will be $\sim 10^{23}$ n cm $^{-2}$ mo $^{-1}$ . Presumably, most materials won't be able to take many months of that. This emphasizes the importance of our design: straight tubes, easily replaced, and not touching one another.

Figure 3.5-25. Reduction in Flux and Power Density as Th Presence in the MCFR(Th) Core Salt Softens the Neutron Energy Spectrum   
![](images/8980cd67a1cd5ba3cff319c3492a1307f9a8fc89b1b95adae21777892cc06255.jpg)  
Bases: 2 m C reflector, $^{233}\mathrm{U} / ^{234}\mathrm{U}$ in core = 7:1, Na/Actinide in core 30/70.

![](images/a9077d51d3bf74c659731eb6fc8c9d4e1bbf6c312c1a58a0696af9c90d0fd4be.jpg)  
Isotopic $\langle \sigma_{\tau} / \sigma_{\tau}\rangle$ in the Core Sal   
Figure 3.5-26. Spectrum Softening of Th Presence in Core Salt: Effect Upon the Fission-to-Capture Rate Competition for Key Isotopes

3.5.9.4 Effect on actinide fission rates and $^{233}\mathrm{U} / ^{234}\mathrm{U}$ ratio. The spectrum softening effect of $\mathrm{ThCl}_4$ content (Fig. 3.5-21) reduces the average fission cross section relative to the capture cross section, especially for even-A isotopes which do not readily fission (Fig. 3.5-26). This favors accumulation of higher-A nuclides which section 2.1.2 showed to be undesirable. Figure 3.5-27 confirms this effect on the $^{233}\mathrm{U} / ^{234}\mathrm{U}$ ratio. Section 3.5.8 showed that $^{234}\mathrm{U}$ presence further softens the spectrum.

$$

# 3.5.10 Reactor Design: Core Carrier Salt (NaCl) Content

Like $\mathrm{ThCl}_4$ , NaCl substitution for $\mathrm{UCl}_3$ in the primary salt significantly reduces the fissile inventory outside the core tubes, but also degrades the neutronic performance in the core. This section analyzes some of these physics consequences assuming a core salt composed of zero $\mathrm{ThCl}_4$ and a $^{233}\mathrm{U}/^{234}\mathrm{U}$ ratio of 7. Results are compared with those from $\mathrm{ThCl}_4$ substitution (Sec. 3.5.9).

3.5.10.1 Effect on BG. NaCl decreases BG through parasitic absorption and moderation of neutrons. Fig. 3.5-28 combines the results from the present MCFR(Th) study with the behavior derived from an MCFR(Pu) study by Taube. The results suggest

1. Generally minimizing the NaCl content   
2. Absolutely avoiding NaCl molar content 80%

Fig. 3.5-29 compares NaCl substitution relative to ThCl₄ substitution on an expanded scale. One sees that ThCl₄ diminishes BG slightly less than NaCl does. The probable reason is that most neutron capture in ThCl₄ contributes to BG (even considering the four Cl atoms), whereas none of that in NaCl does. Table 3.5-IX supports this.

With a thinner blanket, the $\mathrm{ThCl}_{4}$ -NaCl difference would probably be much greater.

3.5.10.2 Effect on reactor fissile inventory and doubling time.

Figure 3.5-30 shows the results of this study. Similar to Sec.

![](images/964b7851de6bed0c2f8f039bf5b7625000ee8a4a885eb6e94060717027bc67dd.jpg)

![](images/b1e9ae6926f81e3108124e5f4ffb30f8ffdd6216335f738419b45260d6e0bf70.jpg)  
Fig. 3.5-28 Drastic Falloff in BG at High Carrier Salt Content   
Fig. 3.5-29 BG Comparison of Carrier Salt Substitution to $\mathsf{ThCl}_4$ (Fertile) Salt Substitution

Doubling Time (years)

U Inventories (IT)

10.

![](images/dd85d7522492e44f9faab1752b07867e9ca129d6e5e7cc26c2820c40817ecfd9.jpg)  
Figure 3.5-30. Effect of Carrier Salt Admixture Upon Fissile

all carrier (NaCl) salt

Inventory and Doubling Time

3.5.9.2, NaCl substitution for UCl₃ increases the fissile inventory in the core tubes within the vessel. However, the larger inventory outside the vessel decreases faster. Thus the total inventory (primary circuit plus blanket) decreases with increasing NaCl content.

NaCl substitution also decreases the BG export rate, but only slightly. Unlike ThCl₄ substitution, NaCl substitution continues to decrease DT down through 30% UCl₃ molar content. However, beyond that point Sec. 3.5.10.1 indicates that BG should fall off drastically, with consequent DT increase.

3.5.10.3 Effect on power density and flux levels. Figure 3.5-31 shows how NaCl substitution for UCl₃ decreases the power density and flux levels. However, ThCl₄-substitution causes an even faster decrease. In both cases the blanket composition remains constant. Thus the greater fall-off in flux from adding ThCl₄ to the core salt must stem from the higher microscopic absorption cross section for Th than for Na (Table 3.5-X), resulting in less neutron leakage from the core.

The difference that $\mathrm{ThCl}_4$ makes relative to NaCl might allow the use of a thinner blanket.

3.5.10.4 Effect on $^{233}\mathrm{U} / ^{234}\mathrm{U}$ ratio. Substitution of non-fissile salts for UCl₃ softens the neutron spectrum because it substitutes a source of elastic- and inelastic- scattered neutrons for one of fission neutrons. In a softer spectrum, the rate of $^{234}\mathrm{U}$ (threshold) fission decreases and $^{233}\mathrm{U} (-1/\mathrm{v})$ capture increases. Both

Total Core Flux (a cm-1s-1)

# 3.5.11 Mutation Effects

3.5.11.1 Significance of actinides which emit alphas. Reactor irradiation transmutes actinides into long-lived alpha-emitters: they increase in atomic number (Z) and weight (A) through $(n, \gamma)$ reactions and $\beta$ -decay; they then generally decrease slowly in Z and A through alpha decay, finally becoming a stable nucleus. Alpha emitters are undesirable because

1. They pose a long-lived radiobiological hazard   
2. They produce neutrons through $(\alpha ,n)$ reactions   
3. Some produce neutrons also through spontaneous fission   
4. They destroy reprocessing chemicals

The radiobiological hazard will depend upon

1. The probability of the isotope reaching the public environment in an ingestible form   
2. Its radioactive half-life: very short- and very long-lived isctopes present little danger   
3. The biological-elimination half-life: how long before the body removes it naturally   
4. The chemical affinity of the isotope for specific organs or other members of the body. Those which seek cut bones are labelled carcinogenic (cancer causing)   
5. The available energy per decay   
6. The occurrence of secondary radiations by reactions such as $(\alpha, n)$ for high energy alphas   
7. The quantity of iscope present.

Combination of these factors defines the toxicity of an isotope - just how much of a practical threat it poses. Table 3.5-XI includes a toxicity classification along with other pertinent physics information. The moderate half-lives of Pu plus their early preeminence in the act-ince burnup chains warrant their highly toxic classification. Carcinogenicity further emphasizes avoiding their production.

The problem with $(\alpha, n)$ reactions as well as spontaneous fission neutrons is that neutrons penetrate gamma shielding: in addition to harming workers directly, they can activate the surroundings, presenting longer-term hazards.

Numerous light elements (up through Al and Si) undergo these reactions with alphas. Fig. 3.5-33 shows some relative yields. F, though not included there reacts almost as strongly as Be. F, Li, and Be are candidate materials for (blanket) salt mix; this phenomenon argues against their use unless the neutron signal and hazard are desired for non-proliferation purposes. Section 3.7 mentions the use of F, O, $\mathrm{Hg}$ , and Na with various reprocessing schemes; this phenomenon would discourage their use there somewhat.

The threshold-like behavior of the $(\alpha, n)$ neutron yields per reaction (Fig. 3.5-33) makes isotopes emitting higher-energy alphas particularly hazardous. These include $212_{Bi}$ , $212_{Po}$ , $216_{Po}$ , $220_{Rn}$ , $224_{Ta}$ , $236_{Pu}$ , $242_{Cm}$ , and $244_{Cm}$ . However, when one considers the half-lives and quantities actually present, the principal $(\alpha, n)$ sources are $238_{Pu}$ , $242_{Cm}$ , and $239_{Pu}$ while the main S. F. neutron sources tend to be $242_{Cm}$ , $244_{Cm}$ , $238_{Pu}$ , $240_{Pu}$ , and $242_{Pu}$ . These all really emphasize a strong desire to avoid Pu and higher actinides.

Figure 3.5-33 Energy-Dependence of Major $(\alpha, n)$ Reactions [137]

3.5.11.2 Significance of $^{232}\mathrm{U}$ Production. Section 3.5.4.2 derived the equations for producing $^{232}\mathrm{U}$ and its daughter (principally $^{208}\mathrm{Tl}$ ) radioactivity. Figure 3.5-34 shows the resulting $^{232}\mathrm{U}$ enrichment in core and blanket salts assuming the constant $^{232}\mathrm{Th}/^{233}\mathrm{U}$ ratios indicated. The time to reach equilibrium concentration in the core reflects the time constant of $1/\sigma\phi = 10$ mos. estimated in Sec. 3.5.4.2. The several-magnitude higher $^{232}\mathrm{U}$ concentration in the core compared to blanket reflects the 1000-higher production coefficient $\alpha_{23\mathrm{N}}^{\prime}$ in that harder neutron spectrum. This means that fresh blanket U will contain not only far less fission product hazard but also far less $^{232}\mathrm{U}$ hazard. Thus it constitutes the biggest threat to proliferation. It is therefore very important to mix it with irradiated core U so as to "spike it" into a less proliferation-hazardous form.

![](images/1ef3cd7ef286e19bac4740a20e231936584e53421cf0e416c23ec18678d33a04.jpg)  
Figure 3.5-34. Buildup of $^{232}\mathrm{U}$ in Core and Blanket Salts

We would like to estimate what level of radiation hazard this $232_{\mathrm{U}}$ daughter activity actually poses to theft, diversion, and weapons manufacture. The minimum critical mass or $^{233}\mathrm{U}$ for weapons use will nominally be $6 \, \mathrm{kg} \, [138]$ . With a U metal density of $19.2 \, \mathrm{g/cm}^3$ [50], that implies a radius of $4.2 \, \mathrm{cm}$ . The corresponding flux from a sphere of such material is given [139] by

$$
\phi = \frac {S}{4 a} [ 2 R a - (a ^ {2} - R ^ {2}) \ln [ (R - a) / (a - R) ] \quad f o r a \geq R
$$

where $S =$ uniform source rate $\equiv \lambda_{18} N_{18}$ (see Eq. 3.5.4-8)

$\equiv$ radial distance from sphere center

$\mathbb{R} =$ sphere radius

For $a = R$

$$
\phi = \frac {\sin}{2}
$$

At a distance of 1 meter from the sphere

$$
\phi = \frac {S / 4}{R + 1 0 0} \left[ 2 R (1 0 0 + R) - \left(R ^ {2} + 2 0 C R + 1 0 ^ {4} - R ^ {2}\right) \ln \left(\frac {2 R + 1 0 0}{1 0 0}\right) \right]
$$

For 2.6-MeV photons the dose conversion factor is $3.6 \times 10^{-6}$ Rem/hr per unit $\gamma$ flux [81]. Then the dose

$$
\begin{array}{r l} D \left(\frac {\mathrm {R e m}}{\mathrm {h r}}\right) & = 3. 6 \times 1 0 ^ {- 6} \times \left(\frac {4 . 2 \mathrm {c m}}{2}\right) \times 0. 0 4 9 \left[ \frac {\mathrm {N} _ {2 2}}{\mathrm {N} _ {2 3}} \right] \times \\ & \left\{\frac {1}{\lambda_ {1 8} \lambda_ {3 2} \lambda_ {0 8}} - \sum_ {i = 1} ^ {3} F _ {i} \right\} \\ & = 3. 7 \times 1 0 ^ {- 7} \left[ \frac {\mathrm {N} _ {2 2}}{\mathrm {N} _ {2 3}} \right] \left\{\frac {1}{\lambda_ {1 8} \lambda_ {3 2} \lambda_ {0 8}} - \sum_ {i = 1} ^ {3} F _ {i} \right\} \end{array}
$$

$$
= 4. 0 \times 1 0 ^ {- 1 0} \left[ \frac {N _ {2 2}}{N _ {2 3}} \right] \left\{\frac {1}{\lambda_ {1 8} \lambda_ {3 2} \lambda_ {0 8}} - \sum_ {i = 1} ^ {3} F _ {i} \right\} \text {f o r a = 1 m}
$$

From Figure 3.5-34 $\left[\mathrm{N}_{22} / \mathrm{N}_{23}\right]$ is typically $10^{-3}$ . Fig. 3.5-35 presents the results for D. One sees that after 10 years the dose from 208Tl approaches

$$
D (\text {s u r f a c e}) = 4 5 \text {R e m / h o u r}
$$

$$
D (1 \text {m e t e r}) = 4 5 \text {m e m / h o u r}
$$

To remove most of this and other radiation one could chemically separate the uranium in the core salt from the $^{208}\mathrm{Tl}$ and all other actinides and fission products. However, this would not separate $^{233}\mathrm{U}$ from $^{232}\mathrm{U}$ . Then, in only two weeks the surface dose would again reach 1 Rem/hour. Furthermore, if only $99\%$ removal efficiency was achieved, a $\frac{1}{2}$ Rem/hour surface dose from $^{208}\mathrm{Tl}$ alone would be present in the interim.

From this one concludes that a highly efficient scientific group with laboratory facilities (such as a national laboratory) might be able to make a bomb without getting a deadly radiation dose, but all others would encounter difficulty.

In addition to these complications, the tell-tale $^{208}\mathrm{Tl}$ signals would continue to emanate from the separated waste as well as from the new regenerating source in the separated U. All these troubles must discourage subnational groups which contemplate taking and using $^{233}\mathrm{U}$ for weapons purposes.

![](images/01dc40e8cfe15ee451302a628ccf0c653c98e7e996aa86e8ff2815377847b5e0.jpg)  
Figure 3.5-35. Variation of Radiation Hazard from $0.1\%$ $^{232}\mathrm{U}$ Daughter Content in $6\mathrm{kg}$ of $^{233}\mathrm{U}$ Following Reprocessing

The effect of such activity on a conventional solid fuel cycle, would probably be to require costly shielding and remote handling to protect the workers. This means in fuel fabrication plants and, possibly, at the end stages of processing plants. A molten salt fuel cycle automates the reprocessing far more; the shorter time out-of-core should also reduce the activity due to neutron absorption reactions with each member of the decay chain.

Meanwhile, in any breeding gain set aside, the radiation hazard will start adding to that from fission products and actinides and increase for several years. Thus the biggest problem which $^{232}\mathrm{U}$ presents in a molten salt thorium cycle should be transport of breeding gain: radiation will make the fuel unwieldy (heavy shielding and remote handling) and hazardous, thereby somewhat unattractive to diversion and to weapons manufacture by subnational groups.

Since $^{232}\mathrm{U}$ and $^{233}\mathrm{U}$ are chemically inseparable this radiation hazard will continue to manifest itself for many multiples of the $^{232}\mathrm{U}$ 72-year halflife, i.e. for centuries. By leaving the breeding gain in chloride form it is ready to startup another plant, without intermediate handling.

# 3.5.11.3 Impact of fission product concentration upon neutron physics

performance. ANISN calculations showed that a FP concentration of $5.8 \times 10^{-4}$ a cm $^{-1}$ b $^{-1}$ ≈ 0.10 [U $_{\text{fissile}}$ ] in an MCFR(Th) reduces the 3R by 0.007 out of 1.34 or 0.5%. Within the accuracy of the calculation, this amount of FP did not affect the critical dimensions.

Figure 3.5-36 shows the FP effect on BG calculated by Taube et al for an MCFR(Pu): $[FP] = 2 \times 10^{-4} = 0.10[\mathrm{Pu}]$ appears to cause a similar ER loss of $0.01 / 1.45 = 0.7\%$ . $[FP] = 1.0[\mathrm{Pu}]$ seems to produce a (proportionally) larger loss of about $10\%$ in BR.

# 3.5.12 Summary

Section 3.5 attempted a first cut at optimizing the reactor design and composition and also analyzed some of the consequences therefrom. The first six subsections defined methods, data, and models.

Sec. 3.5.7 discouraged the use of an inner blanket and showed that two meters constitutes an infinite outer blanket. In that event a reactor reflector contributes little. In addition to achieving maximum BG, the 2 m-blanket greatly reduces the flux dose to the reactor vessel. Sec. 3.5.7 also favored graphite as the core tube wall material because Mo strongly absorbs neutrons.

Section 3.5.8 showed that design decisions which favor a high $233_{\mathrm{U}} / 234_{\mathrm{U}}$ ratio in the core salt produce

1. A hard neutron spectrum there

2. Higher BG and lower DT

3. Minimal presence of Pu and other high-A actinides Fortunately, within the ranges of interest, all the trade studies indicate that the $^{233}\mathrm{U} / ^{234}\mathrm{U}$ ratio on equilibrium fuel cycle will exceed 7. That is sufficiently high to achieve the above goals; additional major efforts are unwarranted.

Sections 3.5.9 and 3.5.10 revealed that both $\mathrm{ThCl}_4$ and $\mathrm{NaCl}$ , acting as diluents in the core salt, greatly reduce flux levels and the amount of out-of-core fissile inventory. They also reduce BG. The combined effects of inventory and BG produce an optimum DT near a U molar composition of about $30\%$ and a Th/U ratio of 1-2. This and the lower flux levels explain the preference of other MCFR

studies for a fissile molar content of $15 - 30\%$ . However, low fissile content will decrease the $^{233}\mathrm{U} / ^{234}\mathrm{U}$ ratio, an indication of greater transplutonium buildup. Thus the true optimum remains to be found and will depend on final assignment of priorities.

DT of 20-30 years appear feasible. This is high compared to quoted DT 13-20 years for an MSBR on the Th fuel cycle. However, an MCFR(Th) should build up less transplutonium.

Efforts to lower DT should focus on the out-of-core inventory. Note also, that, compared to conventional reactors, the absence of fuel fabrication and decladding operations and the minimal transportation and storage needs provide additional fuel inventory advantages which are not taken into account here.

Section 3.5.11.1 quantified somewhat the basis for avoiding transplutonium buildup. Sec. 3.5.11.2 pinpointed fresh blanket U as the greatest proliferation hazard. By immediately mixing it with core salt, one adds not only fission product activity, which is chemically removable, but also $^{232}$ U-daughter radioactivity which is not. The radioactivity will discourage U handling, but not make it impossible. Sec. 3.5.11.3 showed that the presence of too much FP can seriously reduce BG.

# 3.6 Safety & Kinetics

# 3.6.1 Physics of Reactor Safety and Kinetics

3.6.1.1 Effective delayed neutron fraction. Delayed neutrons from fission of $^{233}\mathrm{U}$ , $^{235}\mathrm{U}$ , and $^{239}\mathrm{Pu}$ amount to 0.0027, 0.0065, and 0.0022 of the respective total neutron yields. At first glance this suggests that the effective delayed neutron fraction $/_{\text{eff}}$ for a $^{233}\mathrm{U}$ -fueled MCFR exceeds that for a $^{239}\mathrm{Pu}$ -fueled LIFBR. However, most of the MCFR fuel lies cut-of-core in the primary circuit. This makes $/_{\text{eff}}$ much smaller.

Passing through a core less than 2 meters high, with a velocity of 9 meters per second, the fuel is present for only 0.2 seconds. Each decay half-life of the six delayed-neutron groups (Table 3.6-I) far exceeds that. Therefore the delayed neutrons originate near uniformly throughout the primary circuit and / $\beta$ eff diminishes by the ratio of fuel in-core to that in the total circuit: by 0.2-0.3 to $\beta_{\text{eff}} = 0.0005 - 0.0008$ .

3.6.1.2 Prompt neutron lifetime,1. I increases with the median fission energy $E_{\mathrm{m}}$ , ranging from $5 \times 10^{-7}$ for an LTFBR (190 keV) to $3 \times 10^{-9}$ for Jezebel and $^{233}\mathrm{U}$ Godiva (near 1.6 keV). For the intermediate MCFR(Th) with $E_{\mathrm{m}} = 400 - 700$ keV, I should $\approx 10^{-8}$ s⁻¹.

3.6.1.3 Temperature coefficient of reactivity. Reactor stability depends on several coefficients of reactivity. In a molten salt reactor, the temperature coefficient due to expansion dominates. In an MCFR the very hard neutron spectrum nullifies any Doppler coefficient of reactivity.

Table 3.6-I $^{233}\mathrm{U}$ Delayed Neutron Characteristics   

<table><tr><td>Group</td><td>Half-life (Sec)</td><td>Relative Abundance (%)</td></tr><tr><td>1</td><td>55.</td><td>9</td></tr><tr><td>2</td><td>21.</td><td>30.</td></tr><tr><td>3</td><td>5.</td><td>25.</td></tr><tr><td>4</td><td>2.</td><td>28.</td></tr><tr><td>5</td><td>0.6</td><td>5.</td></tr><tr><td>6</td><td>0.3</td><td>3.</td></tr></table>

Section 3.3.5.6 indicates a typical temperature coefficient of salt density of $0.0004 \, \text{‰}$ /°C. ANISN calculations (Section 3.5.8.3) reveal that $0.050 \, \text{‰}$ lowers reactivity by $0.034 \, \text{‰}$ k, a coefficient of $0.68 \, \text{‰}$ /°C. Applying the above gives $-27 \times 10^{-5} \frac{\Delta \text{k}}{\text{k}}^{\circ} \text{C}^{-1}$ . Using $\beta_{\text{eff}} = 0.00068$ one gets a strong negative temperature coefficient of $-40 \, \text{‰}$ C.

For comparison, a Pu-fuelled LMFBR typically exhibits $-0.4$ to $-0.5\mathrm{g} / {}^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ [140], assuming $\beta_{\mathrm{eff}} = 0.003$ . Calculation for a 1000 MWe MSBR gave an isothermal temperature coefficient of $-0.9\times 10^{-5}\Delta \mathrm{k / k} / {}^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ [131]. However this comprised a $-4.4\times 10^{-5}\Delta \mathrm{k / k} / {}^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ for the negative Doppler coefficient of thorium and positive coefficients for the thermal spectrum graphite moderator and fuel salt density. The latter stems from a negative salt density coefficient of $-0.03\Delta \mathrm{k / k} / \Delta p / \rho$ for their low-actinide fluoride salt mix.

# 3.6.2 Analysis of Formal Operations

3.6.2.1 Reactor startup. MCFR coolant circuits and salt supply will need preheating just like an KSBR does. A salt of subcritical fuel concentration or even a low-melting non-fuel salt could flow test the circuit. Cne might approach zero power criticality and subsequent rise to power by gradually adding fissile fuel through the clean-up loop to a subcritical fuel salt.

3.6.2.2 Reactor control. A limited study [29] indicated feasible control of reactor and power level by manipulating the secondary circuit conditions: mainly varying the flow rate of salt through the core and heat exchangers. The negative temperature coefficient will hold the salt temperature nearly constant at all power levels. The absence of the heat transfer and fuel pin temperature differentials of a solid-fuel reactor allow this direct response.

3.6.2.3 Reactor stability and inventory ratio. The amount of fuel in the primary circuit outside the core fixes the effective delayed neutron fraction and affects the reactor stability. The British [29] showed that an MCFR(U/Pu) should be stable to small reactivity perturbations if the fraction of primary circuit salt in the core exceeded $25\%$ . Engineering studies of their MCFR designs met this requirement with a margin. The primary circuit flow rate seemed to have little effect upon the delayed neutron concentration in the core.

Table 3.6-11 Fuel Inventory in a 11 Gwth MCFR(Pu) Plant [23]   

<table><tr><td>Specific heat exchanger power
(conservative data)</td><td>1 KJ/cm3</td></tr><tr><td>Total volume of heat exchanger for

Taube analyzed a 11 GJth MCFR(Pu) and inventoried (Table 3.6-II) the fissile fuel salt in the primary circuit. Assuming a power density of 11 MJth/liter and conservative heat exchanger performance, $81\%$ of the fissile fuel salt is out of the core. For a given heat exchanger rating, as the total plant power decreases the inventory in the heat exchanger will also by about $0.3\text{m}^3$ fuel per GJth. Little change occurs in the core or piping. Therefore according to the British analysis, this plant should operate at lower power.

Table 3.6-III compares Taube's results to those of British designs and a 1956 ORIL study. The British designs contain only $56 - 64\%$ , of the primary circuit fuel salt out-of-core, reportedly mostly in the duct work. Unlike Taube but in agreement with the British designs, the ORIL/1956 study also attributed a large portion of the inventory to piping between core and heat exchanger.

Table 3.6-III Volume Distributions in Some MCFR(Pu) Designs
